			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Particulates</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Atmospheric_particulate_matter&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Atmospheric particulate matter">Atmospheric particulate matter</a>)</span></div>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MODAL2_M_AER_RA.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Movie map of distribution of aerosol particles, based on data from the <a href="/wiki/Moderate_Resolution_Imaging_Spectroradiometer" class="mw-redirect" title="Moderate Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer">Moderate Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer</a> (MODIS) on NASA's Terra satellite.<br />
* Green areas show aerosol plumes dominated by larger particles.<br />
* Red areas show aerosol plumes dominated by small particles.<br />
* Yellow areas show where large and small aerosol particles are mixing.<br />
* Gray shows where the sensor did not collect data.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Atmospheric particulate matter</b> – also known as <b>particulate matter</b> (<b>PM</b>) or <b>particulates</b> – are microscopic solid or liquid matter suspended in the Earth's atmosphere. The term <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosol</a> commonly refers to the particulate/air mixture, as opposed to the particulate matter alone.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Sources of particulate matter can be man-made or natural. They have impacts on climate and precipitation that adversely affect human health.</p>
<p>Subtypes of atmospheric particulate matter include suspended particulate matter (SPM), thoracic and respirable particles,<sup id="cite_ref-ncbi.nlm.nih.gov_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncbi.nlm.nih.gov-4">[4]</a></sup> inhalable coarse particles, which are [coarse] <a href="/wiki/Particle" title="Particle">particles</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Diameter" title="Diameter">diameter</a> between 2.5 and 10 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micrometres (μm)</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-www3.epa.gov_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www3.epa.gov-5">[5]</a></sup> fine particles with a diameter of 2.5&#160;μm or less<sup id="cite_ref-www3.epa.gov_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www3.epa.gov-5">[5]</a></sup> PM<sub>2.5</sub> ,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> PM<sub>10</sub> <a href="/wiki/Ultrafine_particle" title="Ultrafine particle">ultrafine particles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Agency_for_Research_on_Cancer" title="International Agency for Research on Cancer">IARC</a> and <a href="/wiki/WHO" class="mw-redirect" title="WHO">WHO</a> designate airborne particulates a <a href="/wiki/List_of_IARC_Group_1_carcinogens" title="List of IARC Group 1 carcinogens">Group 1 carcinogen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Particulates are the deadliest form of <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> due to their ability to penetrate deep into the lungs and blood streams unfiltered, causing permanent <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Mutations" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutations">mutations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heart_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart attacks">heart attacks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Premature_death" class="mw-redirect" title="Premature death">premature death</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-epa.gov_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa.gov-8">[8]</a></sup> In 2013, a study involving 312,944 people in nine European countries revealed that there was no safe level of particulates and that for every increase of 10 μg/m3 in PM<sub>10</sub>, the <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a> rate rose 22%. The smaller PM<sub>2.5</sub> were particularly deadly, with a 36% increase in lung cancer per 10 μg/m3 as it can penetrate deeper into the lungs.<sup id="cite_ref-Lancet71013_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet71013-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources_of_atmospheric_particulate_matter">Sources of atmospheric particulate matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Sources of atmospheric particulate matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some particulates occur naturally, originating from <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dust_storm" title="Dust storm">dust storms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forest_fire" class="mw-redirect" title="Forest fire">forest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a> fires, living vegetation, and <a href="/wiki/Sea_spray" title="Sea spray">sea spray</a>. Human activities, such as the burning of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> in vehicles,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Power_station" title="Power station">power plants</a> and various industrial processes, also generate significant amounts of particulates. Coal combustion in developing countries is the primary method for heating homes and supplying energy. Because salt spray over the oceans is the overwhelmingly most common form of particulate in the atmosphere, <i>anthropogenic</i> aerosols—those made by human activities—currently account for about 10 percent of the total mass of aerosols in our atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The composition of aerosols and particles depends on their source. Wind-blown <a href="/wiki/Mineral_dust" title="Mineral dust">mineral dust</a><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> tends to be made of mineral <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a> and other material blown from the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a>; this particulate is <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)">light-absorbing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SOA_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SOA-13">[13]</a></sup> Sea salt<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> is considered the second-largest contributor in the global aerosol budget, and consists mainly of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> originated from sea spray; other constituents of atmospheric sea salt reflect the composition of <a href="/wiki/Sea_water" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea water">sea water</a>, and thus include <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, etc. In addition, sea spray aerosols may contain organic compounds, which influence their chemistry.</p>
<p>Secondary particles derive from the <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> of primary gases such as <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_oxide" title="Sulfur oxide">sulfur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxides</a> into <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> (liquid) and <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> (gaseous). The precursors for these aerosols—i.e. the gases from which they originate—may have an anthropogenic origin (from fossil fuel or coal <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a>) and a natural <a href="/wiki/Biogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Biogenic">biogenic</a> origin. In the presence of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, secondary aerosols often take the form of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">ammonium</a> salts; i.e. <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_sulfate" title="Ammonium sulfate">ammonium sulfate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_nitrate" title="Ammonium nitrate">ammonium nitrate</a> (both can be dry or in aqueous <a href="/wiki/Solution" title="Solution">solution</a>); in the absence of ammonia, secondary compounds take an <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acidic</a> form as sulfuric acid (liquid aerosol droplets) and nitric acid (atmospheric gas), all of which may contribute to the health effects of particulates.<sup id="cite_ref-Int_Panis_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Int_Panis-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Secondary sulfate and nitrate aerosols are strong <a href="/wiki/Scattering" title="Scattering">light-scatterers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> This is mainly because the presence of sulfate and nitrate causes the aerosols to increase to a size that scatters light effectively.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">Organic matter</a> (OM) can be either primary or secondary, the latter part deriving from the oxidation of <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">VOCs</a>; organic material in the atmosphere may either be biogenic or <a href="/wiki/Anthropogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthropogenic">anthropogenic</a>. Organic matter influences the atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">radiation</a> field by both scattering and absorption. Another important aerosol type is <a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">elemental carbon</a> (EC, also known as <i><a href="/wiki/Black_carbon" title="Black carbon">black carbon</a></i>, BC): this aerosol type includes strongly light-absorbing material and is thought to yield large positive <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a>. Organic matter and elemental carbon together constitute the <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbonaceous</a> fraction of aerosols.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Secondary organic aerosols (SOAs), tiny "tar balls" resulting from combustion products of internal combustion engines, have been identified as a danger to health.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTSOA_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTSOA-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>The chemical composition of the aerosol directly affects how it interacts with solar radiation. The chemical constituents within the aerosol change the overall <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a>. The refractive index will determine how much light is scattered and absorbed.</p>
<p>The composition of particulate matter that generally causes visual effects such as smog consists of sulfur dioxide, nitrogen oxides, carbon monoxide, mineral dust, organic matter, and elemental carbon also known as black carbon or <a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a>. The particles are hygroscopic due to the presence of sulfur, and SO<sub>2</sub> is converted to sulfate when high humidity and low temperatures are present. This causes the reduced visibility and yellow color.<sup id="cite_ref-Mongolia_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mongolia-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_distribution_of_particulates">Size distribution of particulates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Size distribution of particulates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Aerosol particles of natural origin (such as windblown dust) tend to have a larger radius than human-produced aerosols such as particle pollution. The false-color maps in the third image on this page show where there are natural aerosols, human pollution, or a mixture of both, monthly.</p>
<p>Among the most obvious patterns that the size distribution time series shows is that in the planet’s most southerly latitudes, nearly all the aerosols are large, but in the high northern latitudes, smaller aerosols are very abundant. Most of the Southern Hemisphere is covered by ocean, where the largest source of aerosols is natural sea salt from dried sea spray. Because land is concentrated in the Northern Hemisphere, the amount of small aerosols from fires and human activities is greater there than in the Southern Hemisphere. Over land, patches of large-radius aerosols appear over deserts and arid regions, most prominently, the <a href="/wiki/Sahara_Desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahara Desert">Sahara Desert</a> in north Africa and the Arabian Peninsula, where dust storms are common. Places where human-triggered or natural fire activity is common (land-clearing fires in the Amazon from August–October, for example, or lightning-triggered fires in the forests of northern Canada in Northern Hemisphere summer) are dominated by smaller aerosols. Human-produced (fossil fuel) pollution is largely responsible for the areas of small aerosols over developed areas such as the eastern United States and Europe, especially in their summer.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (December 2014)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Satellite measurements of aerosols, called aerosol optical thickness, are based on the fact that the particles change the way the atmosphere reflects and absorbs visible and infrared light. As shown in the seventh image on this page, an optical thickness of less than 0.1 (palest yellow) indicates a crystal clear sky with maximum visibility, whereas a value of 1 (reddish brown) indicates very hazy conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (December 2014)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Deposition_processes">Deposition processes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Deposition processes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. </tr>
</table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(aerosol_physics)" title="Deposition (aerosol physics)">Deposition (aerosol physics)</a></div>
<p>In general, the smaller and lighter a particle is, the longer it will stay in the air. Larger particles (greater than 10 micrometers in diameter) tend to settle to the ground by gravity in a matter of hours whereas the smallest particles (less than 1 micrometer) can stay in the atmosphere for weeks and are mostly removed by <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a>. <a href="/wiki/Diesel_particulate_matter" class="mw-redirect" title="Diesel particulate matter">Diesel particulate matter</a> is highest near the source of emission.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Any info regarding DPM and the atmosphere, flora, height, and distance from major sources would be useful to determine health effects.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Control_technologies">Control technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Control technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dust_collector" title="Dust collector">Dust collector</a></div>
<p>A complicated blend of solid and liquid particles result in <a href="/wiki/Particulate_matter" class="mw-redirect" title="Particulate matter">particulate matter</a> and these particulate matter emissions are highly regulated in most industrialized countries. Due to <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issue" title="Environmental issue">environmental concerns</a>, most industries are required to operate some kind of dust collection system to control particulate emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> These systems include inertial collectors (<a href="/wiki/Cyclonic_separator" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyclonic separator">cyclonic separators</a>), fabric filter collectors <a href="/wiki/Baghouse" title="Baghouse">(baghouses)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wet_scrubber" title="Wet scrubber">wet scrubbers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_precipitators" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrostatic precipitators">electrostatic precipitators</a>.</p>
<p>Cyclonic separators are useful for removing large, coarse particles and are often employed as a first step or "pre-cleaner" to other more efficient collectors. Well-designed cyclonic separators can be very efficient in removing even fine particulates, and may be operated continuously without requiring frequent shutdowns for maintenance.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dust_collector" title="Dust collector">Fabric filters</a> or baghouses are the most commonly employed in general industry.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> They work by forcing dust laden air through a bag shaped fabric filter leaving the particulate to collect on the outer surface of the bag and allowing the now clean air to pass through to either be exhausted into the atmosphere or in some cases recirculated into the facility. Common fabrics include polyester and fiberglass and common fabric coatings include <a href="/wiki/PTFE" class="mw-redirect" title="PTFE">PTFE</a> (commonly known as Teflon). The excess dust buildup is then cleaned from the bags and removed from the collector.</p>
<p>Wet scrubbers pass the dirty air through a scrubbing solution (usually a mixture of water and other compounds) allowing the particulate to attach to the liquid molecules. Electrostatic precipitators electrically charge the dirty air as it passes through. The now charged air then passes by large electrostatic plates which attract the charged particle in the airstream collecting them and leaving the now clean air to be exhausted or recirculated.</p>
<p>Besides removing particulates from the source of the pollution, it can also be cleaned in open air.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_effects">Climate effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Climate effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Radiative-forcings.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Radiative-forcings.svg/220px-Radiative-forcings.svg.png" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Radiative-forcings.svg/330px-Radiative-forcings.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Radiative-forcings.svg/440px-Radiative-forcings.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Radiative-forcings.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
2005 radiative forcings and uncertainties as estimated by the IPCC.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Atmospheric aerosols affect the climate of the earth by changing the amount of incoming <a href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a> and outgoing terrestrial long wave radiation retained in the earth's system. This occurs through several distinct mechanisms which are split into direct, indirect<sup id="cite_ref-Haywood2000_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haywood2000-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Twomey1977_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Twomey1977-27">[27]</a></sup> and semi-direct aerosol effects. The aerosol climate effects are the biggest source of uncertainty in future climate predictions.<sup id="cite_ref-Forster2007_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forster2007-28">[28]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a>, Third Assessment Report, says: <i>While the radiative forcing due to <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> may be determined to a reasonably high degree of accuracy... the uncertainties relating to aerosol radiative forcings remain large, and rely to a large extent on the estimates from global modelling studies that are difficult to verify at the present time</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aerosol_radiative_effects">Aerosol radiative effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Aerosol radiative effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Modis_aerosol_optical_depth.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Modis_aerosol_optical_depth.png/220px-Modis_aerosol_optical_depth.png" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Modis_aerosol_optical_depth.png/330px-Modis_aerosol_optical_depth.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Modis_aerosol_optical_depth.png/440px-Modis_aerosol_optical_depth.png 2x" data-file-width="2413" data-file-height="1352" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Modis_aerosol_optical_depth.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Global aerosol <a href="/wiki/Optical_thickness" class="mw-redirect" title="Optical thickness">optical thickness</a>. The aerosol scale (yellow to dark reddish-brown) indicates the relative amount of particles that absorb sunlight.</div>
</div>
</div>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_effect">Direct effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Direct effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cloud_3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Cloud_3.JPG/250px-Cloud_3.JPG" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Cloud_3.JPG/375px-Cloud_3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Cloud_3.JPG/500px-Cloud_3.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cloud_3.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Particulates in the air causing shades of grey and pink in <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a> during sunset</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The direct aerosol effect consists of any direct interaction of radiation with atmospheric aerosol, such as absorption or scattering. It affects both short and longwave radiation to produce a net negative radiative forcing.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The magnitude of the resultant radiative forcing due to the direct effect of an aerosol is dependent on the <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> of the underlying surface, as this affects the net amount of radiation absorbed or scattered to space. e.g. if a highly scattering aerosol is above a surface of low albedo it has a greater radiative forcing than if it was above a surface of high albedo. The converse is true of absorbing aerosol, with the greatest radiative forcing arising from a highly absorbing aerosol over a surface of high albedo.<sup id="cite_ref-Haywood2000_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haywood2000-26">[26]</a></sup> The direct aerosol effect is a first order effect and is therefore classified as a radiative forcing by the <a href="/wiki/IPCC" class="mw-redirect" title="IPCC">IPCC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Forster2007_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forster2007-28">[28]</a></sup> The interaction of an aerosol with radiation is quantified by the <a href="/wiki/Single-scattering_albedo" title="Single-scattering albedo">single-scattering albedo</a> (SSA), the ratio of scattering alone to scattering plus absorption (<i>extinction</i>) of radiation by a particle. The SSA tends to unity if scattering dominates, with relatively little absorption, and decreases as absorption increases, becoming zero for infinite absorption. For example, sea-salt aerosol has an SSA of 1, as a sea-salt particle only scatters, whereas soot has an SSA of 0.23, showing that it is a major atmospheric aerosol absorber.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indirect_effect">Indirect effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Indirect effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Indirect aerosol effect consists of any change to the earth's radiative budget due to the modification of clouds by atmospheric aerosols, and consists of several distinct effects. Cloud droplets form onto pre-existing aerosol particles, known as <a href="/wiki/Cloud_condensation_nuclei" title="Cloud condensation nuclei">cloud condensation nuclei</a> (CCN).</p>
<p>For any given meteorological conditions, an increase in CCN leads to an increase in the number of cloud droplets. This leads to more scattering of shortwave radiation i.e. an increase in the albedo of the cloud, known as the Cloud albedo effect, First indirect effect or <a href="/wiki/Twomey_effect" title="Twomey effect">Twomey effect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Twomey1977_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Twomey1977-27">[27]</a></sup> Evidence supporting the cloud albedo effect has been observed from the effects of ship exhaust plumes<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Biomass_burning" class="mw-redirect" title="Biomass burning">biomass burning</a><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> on cloud albedo compared to ambient clouds. The Cloud albedo aerosol effect is a first order effect and therefore classified as a radiative forcing by the <a href="/wiki/IPCC" class="mw-redirect" title="IPCC">IPCC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Forster2007_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forster2007-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>An increase in cloud droplet number due to the introduction of aerosol acts to reduce the cloud droplet size, as the same amount of water is divided between more droplets. This has the effect of suppressing precipitation, increasing the cloud lifetime, known as the cloud lifetime aerosol effect, second indirect effect or Albrecht effect.<sup id="cite_ref-Forster2007_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forster2007-28">[28]</a></sup> This has been observed as the suppression of drizzle in ship exhaust plume compared to ambient clouds,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> and inhibited precipitation in biomass burning plumes.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> This cloud lifetime effect is classified as a climate feedback (rather than a radiative forcing) by the <a href="/wiki/IPCC" class="mw-redirect" title="IPCC">IPCC</a> due to the interdependence between it and the hydrological cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-Forster2007_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forster2007-28">[28]</a></sup> However, it has previously been classified as a negative radiative forcing.<sup id="cite_ref-Hansen1997_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen1997-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Semi-direct_effect">Semi-direct effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Semi-direct effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Semi-direct effect concerns any radiative effect caused by absorbing atmospheric aerosol such as soot, apart from direct scattering and absorption, which is classified as the direct effect. It encompasses many individual mechanisms, and in general is more poorly defined and understood than the direct and indirect aerosol effects. For instance, if absorbing aerosols are present in a layer aloft in the atmosphere, they can heat surrounding air which inhibits the condensation of water vapour, resulting in less cloud formation.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Additionally, heating a layer of the atmosphere relative to the surface results in a more stable atmosphere due to the inhibition of atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a>. This inhibits the convective uplift of moisture,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> which in turn reduces cloud formation. The heating of the atmosphere aloft also leads to a cooling of the surface, resulting in less evaporation of surface water. The effects described here all lead to a reduction in cloud cover i.e. an increase in planetary albedo. The semi-direct effect classified as a climate feedback) by the <a href="/wiki/IPCC" class="mw-redirect" title="IPCC">IPCC</a> due to the interdependence between it and the hydrological cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-Forster2007_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forster2007-28">[28]</a></sup> However, it has previously been classified as a negative radiative forcing.<sup id="cite_ref-Hansen1997_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen1997-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roles_of_different_aerosol_species">Roles of different aerosol species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Roles of different aerosol species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sulfate_aerosol">Sulfate aerosol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Sulfate aerosol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stratospheric_sulfur_aerosols" title="Stratospheric sulfur aerosols">stratospheric sulfur aerosols</a></div>
<p>Sulfate aerosol has two main effects, direct and indirect. The direct effect, via <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>, is a cooling effect that slows the overall rate of <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>: the IPCC's best estimate of the radiative forcing is −0.4 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a> per square meter with a range of −0.2 to −0.8 W/m²<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> but there are substantial uncertainties. The effect varies strongly geographically, with most cooling believed to be at and downwind of major industrial centres. Modern <a href="/wiki/Climate_model" title="Climate model">climate models</a> addressing the <a href="/wiki/Attribution_of_recent_climate_change" title="Attribution of recent climate change">attribution of recent climate change</a> take into account sulfate forcing, which appears to account (at least partly) for the slight drop in global temperature in the middle of the 20th century. The indirect effect (via the aerosol acting as cloud condensation nuclei, <a href="/wiki/Cloud_condensation_nuclei" title="Cloud condensation nuclei">CCN</a>, and thereby modifying the cloud properties -albedo and lifetime-) is more uncertain but is believed to be a cooling.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Black_carbon">Black carbon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Black carbon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Black carbon (BC), or carbon black, or elemental carbon (EC), often called soot, is composed of pure carbon clusters, skeleton balls and <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">buckyballs</a>, and is one of the most important absorbing aerosol species in the atmosphere. It should be distinguished from organic carbon (OC): clustered or aggregated organic molecules on their own or permeating an EC buckyball. BC from fossil fuels is estimated by the IPCC in the Fourth Assessment Report of the IPCC, 4AR, to contribute a global mean radiative forcing of +0.2 W/m² (was +0.1 W/m² in the Second Assessment Report of the IPCC, SAR), with a range +0.1 to +0.4 W/m². Bond et al., however, states that "the best estimate for the industrial-era (1750 to 2005) direct radiative forcing of atmospheric black carbon is +0.71 W/m² with 90% uncertainty bounds of (+0.08, +1.27) W/m²" with "total direct forcing by all black carbon sources, without subtracting the preindustrial background, is estimated as +0.88 (+0.17, +1.48) W/m²"<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Instances_of_aerosol_affecting_climate">Instances of aerosol affecting climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Instances of aerosol affecting climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mauna_Loa_atmospheric_transmission.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Mauna_Loa_atmospheric_transmission.png/350px-Mauna_Loa_atmospheric_transmission.png" width="350" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Mauna_Loa_atmospheric_transmission.png/525px-Mauna_Loa_atmospheric_transmission.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9c/Mauna_Loa_atmospheric_transmission.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mauna_Loa_atmospheric_transmission.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Solar radiation reduction due to volcanic eruptions</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Volcanoes are a large natural source of aerosol and have been linked to changes in the earth's climate often with consequences for the human population. Eruptions linked to changes in climate include the 1600 eruption of <a href="/wiki/Huaynaputina" title="Huaynaputina">Huaynaputina</a> which was linked to the <a href="/wiki/Russian_famine_of_1601_-_1603" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian famine of 1601 - 1603">Russian famine of 1601 - 1603</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Geology_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geology-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> leading to the deaths of two million, and the 1991 eruption of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Pinatubo" title="Mount Pinatubo">Mount Pinatubo</a> which caused a global cooling of approximately 0.5&#160;°C lasting several years.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Research tracking the effect of light-scattering aerosols in the stratosphere during 2000 and 2010 and comparing its pattern to volcanic activity show a close correlation. Simulations of the effect of anthropogenic particles showed little influence at present levels.<sup id="cite_ref-SN03413_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SN03413-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-grl.50263_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grl.50263-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aerosols are also thought to affect weather and climate on a regional scale. The failure of the Indian Monsoon has been linked to the suppression of evaporation of water from the Indian Ocean due to the semi-direct effect of anthropogenic aerosol.<sup id="cite_ref-Chung2006_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chung2006-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recent studies of the <a href="/wiki/Sahel_drought" title="Sahel drought">Sahel drought</a><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> and major increases since 1967 in rainfall over the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory" title="Northern Territory">Northern Territory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kimberley_(Western_Australia)" title="Kimberley (Western Australia)">Kimberley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pilbara" title="Pilbara">Pilbara</a> and around the <a href="/wiki/Nullarbor_Plain" title="Nullarbor Plain">Nullarbor Plain</a> have led some scientists to conclude that the aerosol haze over <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> has been steadily shifting tropical rainfall in both hemispheres southward.<sup id="cite_ref-Chung2006_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chung2006-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>The latest studies of severe rainfall declines over <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_Australia" title="Climate change in Australia">southern Australia</a> since 1997<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> have led climatologists there to consider the possibility that these Asian aerosols have shifted not only tropical but also midlatitude systems southward.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_effects">Health effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Health effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><span id="Health_effects"></span></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Luftguete_messstation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Luftguete_messstation.jpg/220px-Luftguete_messstation.jpg" width="220" height="392" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Luftguete_messstation.jpg/330px-Luftguete_messstation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Luftguete_messstation.jpg/440px-Luftguete_messstation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="859" data-file-height="1530" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Luftguete_messstation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Air pollution measurement station in <a href="/wiki/Emden" title="Emden">Emden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_coal_industry" title="Environmental impact of the coal industry">Environmental impact of the coal industry</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size.2C_shape_and_solubility_matter">Size, shape and solubility matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Size, shape and solubility matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The size of the particle is a main determinant of where in the respiratory tract the particle will come to rest when inhaled. Larger particles are generally filtered in the <a href="/wiki/Human_nose" title="Human nose">nose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Throat" title="Throat">throat</a> via cilia and mucus, but particulate matter smaller than about 10 micrometers, can settle in the bronchi and <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a> and cause health problems. The 10 micrometer size does not represent a strict boundary between respirable and non-respirable particles, but has been agreed upon for monitoring of airborne particulate matter by most regulatory agencies. Because of their small size, particles on the order of 10 micrometers or less (PM<sub>10</sub>) can penetrate the deepest part of the lungs such as the bronchioles or alveoli.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="PM2.5"></span>Similarly, so called fine PM, (often referred to as <i>PM<sub>2.5</sub></i>), tend to penetrate into the <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">gas exchange</a> regions of the lung (alveolus), and very small particles (&lt; 100 nanometers) may pass through the lungs to affect other organs. Penetration of particles is not wholly dependent on their size; shape and chemical composition also play a part. To avoid this complication, simple nomenclature is used to indicate the different degrees of relative penetration of a PM particle into the <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiovascular">cardiovascular</a> system. <b>Inhalable particles</b> penetrate no further than the <a href="/wiki/Bronchi" class="mw-redirect" title="Bronchi">bronchi</a> as they are filtered out by the <a href="/wiki/Cilia" class="mw-redirect" title="Cilia">cilia</a>. <b>Thoracic particles</b> can penetrate right into terminal <a href="/wiki/Bronchioles" class="mw-redirect" title="Bronchioles">bronchioles</a> whereas PM which can penetrate to <a href="/wiki/Alveoli" class="mw-redirect" title="Alveoli">alveoli</a>, the gas exchange area, and hence the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a> are termed <b>respirable particles</b>. In analogy, the inhalable dust fraction is the fraction of dust entering nose and mouth which may be deposited anywhere in the respiratory tract. The thoracic fraction is the fraction that enters the thorax and is deposited within the lung's airways. The respirable fraction is what is deposited in the gas exchange regions (alveoli).<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The smallest particles, less than 100 nanometers (<a href="/wiki/Nanoparticles" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanoparticles">nanoparticles</a>), may be even more damaging to the cardiovascular system.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Nanoparticles can pass through cell membranes and migrate into other organs, including the brain. Particles emitted from modern <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel engines</a> (commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/Diesel_Particulate_Matter" class="mw-redirect" title="Diesel Particulate Matter">Diesel Particulate Matter</a>, or DPM) are typically in the size range of 100 nanometers (0.1 micrometer). These <a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a> particles also carry <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogens</a> like <a href="/wiki/Benzopyrene" title="Benzopyrene">benzopyrenes</a> adsorbed on their surface. Particulate mass is not a proper measure of the health hazard, because one particle of 10&#160;µm diameter has approximately the same mass as 1 million particles of 100&#160;nm diameter, but is much less hazardous, as it unlikely to enter the alveoli. Legislative limits for engine emissions based on mass are therefore not protective. Proposals for new regulations exist in some countries,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (September 2014)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> with suggestions to limit the particle <i>surface area</i> or the <i>particle count</i> (numerical quantity) instead.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The site and extent of absorption of inhaled gases and vapors are determined by their solubility in water. Absorption is also dependent upon air flow rates and the partial pressure of the gases in the inspired air. The fate of a specific contaminant is dependent upon the form in which it exists (aerosol or particulate). Inhalation also depends upon the breathing rate of the subject.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another complexity not entirely documented is how the shape of PM can affect health, except for the needle-like shape of <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a> which can lodge itself in the lungs. Geometrically angular shapes have more surface area than rounder shapes, which in turn affects the binding capacity of the particle to other, possibly more dangerous substances.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_problems">Health problems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Health problems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Py%C5%82_zawieszony_Komunikat_o_jako%C5%9Bci_powietrza_w_Katowicach_7.10.2011_godz._9.10.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Py%C5%82_zawieszony_Komunikat_o_jako%C5%9Bci_powietrza_w_Katowicach_7.10.2011_godz._9.10.JPG/220px-Py%C5%82_zawieszony_Komunikat_o_jako%C5%9Bci_powietrza_w_Katowicach_7.10.2011_godz._9.10.JPG" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Py%C5%82_zawieszony_Komunikat_o_jako%C5%9Bci_powietrza_w_Katowicach_7.10.2011_godz._9.10.JPG/330px-Py%C5%82_zawieszony_Komunikat_o_jako%C5%9Bci_powietrza_w_Katowicach_7.10.2011_godz._9.10.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Py%C5%82_zawieszony_Komunikat_o_jako%C5%9Bci_powietrza_w_Katowicach_7.10.2011_godz._9.10.JPG/440px-Py%C5%82_zawieszony_Komunikat_o_jako%C5%9Bci_powietrza_w_Katowicach_7.10.2011_godz._9.10.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4475" data-file-height="3057" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Py%C5%82_zawieszony_Komunikat_o_jako%C5%9Bci_powietrza_w_Katowicach_7.10.2011_godz._9.10.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Air quality information on PM10 displayed in <a href="/wiki/Katowice" title="Katowice">Katowice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The effects of inhaling particulate matter that have been widely studied in humans and animals include <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>, lung cancer, cardiovascular disease, respiratory diseases, <a href="/wiki/Premature_delivery" class="mw-redirect" title="Premature delivery">premature delivery</a>, birth defects, and premature death.</p>
<p>Increased levels of fine particles in the air as a result of <i>anthropogenic</i> particulate air pollution "is consistently and independently related to the most serious effects, including <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a><sup id="cite_ref-Lancet71013_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet71013-9">[9]</a></sup> and other <a href="/wiki/Cardiopulmonary" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiopulmonary">cardiopulmonary</a> <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">mortality</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The large number of deaths<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and other health problems associated with particulate pollution was first demonstrated in the early 1970s<sup id="cite_ref-lave_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lave-57">[57]</a></sup> and has been reproduced many times since. PM pollution is estimated to cause 22,000–52,000 deaths per year in the United States (from 2000)<sup id="cite_ref-mokdad_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mokdad-58">[58]</a></sup> contributed to ~370,000 premature deaths in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> during 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-EEA_2005_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EEA_2005-59">[59]</a></sup> and 3.22 million deaths globally in 2010 per the <a href="/wiki/Global_Burden_of_Disease_Study" title="Global Burden of Disease Study">global burden of disease collaboration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lim_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lim-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2002 study indicated that PM<sub>2.5</sub> leads to high plaque deposits in <a href="/wiki/Arteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Arteries">arteries</a>, causing vascular inflammation and <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">atherosclerosis</a> – a hardening of the arteries that reduces elasticity, which can lead to heart attacks and other cardiovascular problems.<sup id="cite_ref-pope_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pope-61">[61]</a></sup> A 2014 meta analysis reported that long term exposure to particulate matter is linked to coronary events. The study included 11 cohorts participating in the European Study of Cohorts for Air Pollution Effects (ESCAPE) with 100,166 participants, followed for an average of 11.5 years. An increase in estimated annual exposure to PM 2.5 of just 5&#160;µg/m<sup>3</sup> was linked with a 13% increased risk of heart attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In 2017 a study revealed that PM not only affects human cells and tissues, but also impacts bacteria which cause disease in humans. <sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> This study concluded that <a href="/wiki/Biofilm" title="Biofilm">biofilm</a> formation, antibiotic tolerance, and colonisation of both <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Staphylococcus aureus">Staphylococcus aureus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Streptococcus_pneumoniae" title="Streptococcus pneumoniae">Streptococcus pneumonia</a>e</i> was altered by <a href="/wiki/Black_carbon" title="Black carbon">Black Carbon</a> exposure.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization (WHO)</a> estimated in 2005 that "... fine particulate air pollution (PM(2.5)), causes about 3% of mortality from cardiopulmonary disease, about 5% of mortality from cancer of the trachea, bronchus, and lung, and about 1% of mortality from acute respiratory infections in children under 5 years, worldwide.".<sup id="cite_ref-who_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-who-64">[64]</a></sup> Short-term exposure at elevated concentrations can significantly contribute to heart disease, as recently described in a 2016 study.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> A 2011 study concluded that traffic exhaust is the single most serious preventable cause of <a href="/wiki/Heart_attack" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart attack">heart attack</a> in the general public, the cause of 7.4% of all attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>The largest US study on acute health effects of coarse particle pollution between 2.5 and 10 micrometers in diameter. was published 2008 and found an association with hospital admissions for cardiovascular diseases but no evidence of an association with the number of hospital admissions for respiratory diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> After taking into account fine particle levels (PM 2.5 and less), the association with coarse particles remained but was no longer statistically significant, which means the effect is due to the subsection of fine particles.</p>
<p>Particulate matter studies in Bangkok Thailand from 2008 indicated a 1.9% increased risk of dying from cardiovascular disease, and 1.0% risk of all disease for every 10 micrograms per cubic meter. Levels averaged 65 in 1996, 68 in 2002, and 52 in 2004. Decreasing levels may be attributed to conversions of diesel to natural gas combustion as well as improved regulations.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Mongolian government agency recorded a 45% increase in the rate of respiratory illness in the past five years (reported in September 2014). Bronchial asthma, chronic obstructive pulmonary disease and interstitial pneumonia were the most common ailments treated by area hospitals. Levels of premature death, chronic bronchitis, and cardiovascular disease are increasing at a rapid rate.<sup id="cite_ref-Mongolia_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mongolia-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_vegetation">Effects on vegetation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Effects on vegetation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Particulate matter can clog stomatal openings of plants and interfere with photosynthesis functions.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In this manner high particulate matter concentrations in the atmosphere can lead to growth stunting or mortality in some plant species.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Due to the highly toxic health effects of particulate matter, most governments have created regulations both for the emissions allowed from certain types of pollution sources (motor vehicles, industrial emissions etc.) and for the ambient concentration of particulates. The <a href="/wiki/International_Agency_for_Research_on_Cancer" title="International Agency for Research on Cancer">IARC</a> and <a href="/wiki/WHO" class="mw-redirect" title="WHO">WHO</a> designates particulates a <a href="/wiki/List_of_IARC_Group_1_carcinogens" title="List of IARC Group 1 carcinogens">Group 1 carcinogen</a>. Particulates are the deadliest form of <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> due to their ability to penetrate deep into the lungs and blood streams unfiltered, causing permanent <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Mutations" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutations">mutations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heart_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart attacks">heart attacks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Premature_death" class="mw-redirect" title="Premature death">premature death</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-epa.gov_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa.gov-8">[8]</a></sup> In 2013, the ESCAPE study involving 312,944 people in nine European countries revealed that there was no safe level of particulates, and that for every increase of 10 μg/m3 in PM10, the lung cancer rate rose 22%. For PM2.5 there was a 36% increase in lung cancer per 10 μg/m3.<sup id="cite_ref-Lancet71013_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet71013-9">[9]</a></sup> In a 2014 metaanalysis of 18 studies globally including the ESCAPE data, for every increase of 10 μg/m3 in PM2.5, the lung cancer rate rose 9%.<sup id="cite_ref-ehp_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ehp-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> the standard for particulate matter is set nationally by the federal-provincial <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Council_of_Ministers_of_the_Environment" title="Canadian Council of Ministers of the Environment">Canadian Council of Ministers of the Environment</a> (CCME). Jurisdictions (provinces) may set more stringent standards. The CCME standard for particulate matter 2.5 (PM2.5) as of 2015 is 28&#160;μg/m<sup>3</sup> (daily average, i.e. 24-hour period, 3-year average, 98th percentile) and 10 μg/m³ (annual mean) .<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> has set limits for particulates in the air:<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a> (EPA) has set standards for PM<sub>10</sub> and PM<sub>2.5</sub> concentrations.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> (See <a href="/wiki/National_Ambient_Air_Quality_Standards" title="National Ambient Air Quality Standards">National Ambient Air Quality Standards</a>)</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="California">California</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: California">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" width="42" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.<br />
<small>Last update: 22 Jan 2009</small></span> <small><i>(September 2016)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In October 2008, the Department of Toxic Substances Control (DTSC), within the <a href="/wiki/California_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="California Environmental Protection Agency">California Environmental Protection Agency</a>, announced its intent to request information regarding analytical test methods, fate and transport in the environment, and other relevant information from manufacturers of carbon nanotubes.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> DTSC is exercising its authority under the California Health and Safety Code, Chapter 699, sections 57018-57020.<sup id="cite_ref-chemical_call_in_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chemical_call_in-89">[89]</a></sup> These sections were added as a result of the adoption of Assembly Bill AB 289 (2006).<sup id="cite_ref-chemical_call_in_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chemical_call_in-89">[89]</a></sup> They are intended to make information on the fate and transport, detection and analysis, and other information on chemicals more available. The law places the responsibility to provide this information to the Department on those who manufacture or import the chemicals.</p>
<p>On 22 January 2009, a formal information request letter<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> was sent to manufacturers who produce or import carbon nanotubes in California, or who may export carbon nanotubes into the State.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> This letter constitutes the first formal implementation of the authorities placed into statute by AB 289 and is directed to manufacturers of carbon nanotubes, both industry and academia within the State, and to manufacturers outside California who export carbon nanotubes to California. This request for information must be met by the manufacturers within one year. DTSC is waiting for the upcoming 22 January 2010 deadline for responses to the data call-in.</p>
<p>The California Nano Industry Network and DTSC hosted a full-day symposium on 16 November 2009 in Sacramento, CA. This symposium provided an opportunity to hear from nanotechnology industry experts and discuss future regulatory considerations in California.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>DTSC is expanding the Specific Chemical Information Call-in to members of the nanometal oxides, the latest information can be found on their website.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Colorado">Colorado</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Colorado">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(August 2012)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Key points in the Colorado Plan include reducing emission levels and solutions by sector. Agriculture, transportation, green electricity, and renewable energy research are the main concepts and goals in this plan. Political programs such as mandatory vehicle emissions testing and the prohibition of smoking indoors are actions taken by local government to create public awareness and participation in cleaner air. The location of Denver next to the Rocky Mountains and wide expanse of plains makes the metro area of Colorado's capital city a likely place for smog and visible air pollution.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Affected_areas">Affected areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Affected areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:448px;max-width:448px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:US-PM2.5-nonattainment-2007-06.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/US-PM2.5-nonattainment-2007-06.png/220px-US-PM2.5-nonattainment-2007-06.png" width="220" height="165" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/US-PM2.5-nonattainment-2007-06.png/330px-US-PM2.5-nonattainment-2007-06.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/US-PM2.5-nonattainment-2007-06.png/440px-US-PM2.5-nonattainment-2007-06.png 2x" data-file-width="1089" data-file-height="816" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">U.S. counties violating national PM<sub>2.5</sub> standards</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:US-PM10-nonattainment-2007-06.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/US-PM10-nonattainment-2007-06.png/220px-US-PM10-nonattainment-2007-06.png" width="220" height="171" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/US-PM10-nonattainment-2007-06.png/330px-US-PM10-nonattainment-2007-06.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/US-PM10-nonattainment-2007-06.png/440px-US-PM10-nonattainment-2007-06.png 2x" data-file-width="1046" data-file-height="815" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">U.S. counties violating national PM<sub>10</sub> standards</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PM10_in_Europe.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/PM10_in_Europe.png/440px-PM10_in_Europe.png" width="440" height="343" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/PM10_in_Europe.png/660px-PM10_in_Europe.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/PM10_in_Europe.png/880px-PM10_in_Europe.png 2x" data-file-width="2049" data-file-height="1598" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PM10_in_Europe.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Concentration of PM<sub>10</sub><sup id="cite_ref-EEA_2005_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EEA_2005-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> in Europe</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The most concentrated particulate matter pollution resulting from the burning of fossil fuels by transportation and industrial sources tends to be in densely populated metropolitan areas in developing countries such as <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia_2">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>PM10 pollution in <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">coal mining</a> areas in Australia such as the <a href="/wiki/Latrobe_Valley" title="Latrobe Valley">Latrobe Valley</a> in Victoria and the <a href="/wiki/Hunter_Region" title="Hunter Region">Hunter Region</a> in New South Wales significantly increased during 2004 to 2014. Although the increase did not significantly add to non-attainment statistics the rate of increase has risen each year during 2010 to 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian4115_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian4115-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China_2">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some cities in Northern China and South Asia have had concentrations above 200&#160;µg/m<sup>3</sup> up to a few years ago<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (December 2014)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The PM levels in Chinese cities have been extreme in recent years<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (December 2014)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup>, reaching an all-time high in Beijing on 12 January 2013, of 993&#160;µg/m<sup>3</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mongolia_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mongolia-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>To monitor the air quality of south China, the U.S. Consulate <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> set a PM 2.5 monitor on <a href="/wiki/Shamian_Island" title="Shamian Island">Shamian Island</a> in Guangzhou, and displays readings on its official website and social platforms.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ulaanbaatar">Ulaanbaatar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Ulaanbaatar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>'s capital city <a href="/wiki/Ulaanbaatar" title="Ulaanbaatar">Ulaanbaatar</a> has an annual average mean temperature of about 0&#160;°C, making it the world's coldest capital city. About 40% of the population lives in apartments, 80% of which are supplied with central heating systems from 3 combined heat and power plants. In 2007, the power plants consumed almost 3.4 million tons of coal. The pollution control technology is in poor condition.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The other 60% of the population reside in shantytowns (Ger districts), which have developed due to the country's new market economy and the very cold winter seasons. The poor in these districts cook and heat their wood houses with indoor stoves fueled by wood or coal. The resulting air pollution is characterized by raised sulphur dioxide and nitrogen oxide levels and very high concentrations of airborne particles and <a href="/wiki/Particulate_matter" class="mw-redirect" title="Particulate matter">particulate matter</a> (PM).<sup id="cite_ref-Mongolia_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mongolia-19">[19]</a></sup> Annual seasonal average particulate matter concentrations have been recorded as high as 279&#160;µg/m<sup>3</sup> (micrograms per cubic meter).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The World Health Organization's recommended annual mean PM<sub>10</sub> level is 20&#160;µg/m<sup>3</sup>,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> which means that Ulaanbaatar's PM<sub>10</sub> annual mean levels are 14 times higher than recommended, and that it has left Northern China's most polluted cities in its wake.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>During the winter months in particular, the air pollution obscures the air, affecting the visibility in the city to such an extent that airplanes on some occasions are prevented from landing at the airport.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In addition to stack emissions, another source unaccounted for in the emission inventory is <a href="/wiki/Fly_ash" title="Fly ash">fly ash</a> from ash ponds, the final disposal place for fly ash that has been collected in settling tanks. Ash ponds are continually eroded by wind during the dry season.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Particulates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

"&#160;:"… [O]dds ratios and frequencies of each trigger were used to compute population-attributable fractions (PAFs), which estimate the proportion of cases that could be avoided if a risk factor were removed. PAFs depend not only on the risk factor strength at the individual level but also on its frequency in the community. ... [T]he exposure prevalence for triggers in the relevant control time window ranged from 0.04% for cocaine use to 100% for air pollution. ... Taking into account the OR and the prevalences of exposure, the highest PAF was estimated for traffic exposure (7.4%) ...</span></li>

<th scope="row" class="navbox-group">Health and safety</th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px;text-align: middle;">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
